*The language of the Buddhas is without variance—*  
*Only One Vehicle without a second.*  

*In past countless eons,*  
*Countless, immeasurable Buddhas have passed away,*  
*A hundred thousand billion types,*  
*Beyond measure in number.*  

*All such World-Honored Ones,*  
*Through various causes, conditions, and parables,*  
*Immeasurable skillful means-power,*  
*Expounded the characteristics of various dharmas.*  

*All these World-Honored Ones,*  
*All taught the one-vehicle dharma,*  
*Transforming countless sentient beings,*  
*Causing them to enter the Buddha path.*  

*Again, all the great sage-rulers,*  
*Knowing all the world—*  
*Devas, humans, and all other living kinds,*  
*The deep desires of their hearts—*  

*By means of alternative skillful means,*  
*They further reveal the supreme reality.[^32]*  
*If there are sentient beings,*  
*Who encounter the Buddhas of the past,*  

*Or hearing the dharma of giving,*  
*Or maintaining precepts, practicing patience,*  
*Exerting effort, meditation, and wisdom—*  
*Cultivating all manner of virtue and good fortune—*  

*All such persons,*  
*Have already become Buddhas.*  
*After the Buddhas have passed away,*  
*If beings possess gentle, soft hearts,*  

*All such sentient beings,*  
*Have already become Buddhas.*  
*After the Buddhas have passed away,*  
*Those who make offerings to the relics,*  

*Erecting billions of kinds of stūpas,*  
*Of gold, silver, and crystal,*  
*Beryl and agate,*  
*Pearls and jewels—*  

*Brightly adorned with purity,*  
*Decorated and ornate with many stūpas,*  
*Or building stone temples,*  
*Sandalwood and aloe-wood,*  

*Timber and other materials,*  
*Brick, tile, clay and earth—*  
*Or in wastelands,*  
*Accumulating earth into Buddha shrines,*  

*Even children at play,*  
*Piling sand to make Buddha stūpas—*  
*All such persons as these,*  
*Have already become Buddhas.*  

*If a person, for the Buddha's sake,*  
*Establishes all manner of Buddha images,*  
*Carving and sculpting all kinds of forms—*  
*All have already become Buddhas.*  

*Made from the seven precious substances,*  
*Copper, stone, red and white bronze,*  
*White lead and tin,*  
*Wood and clay, etc.—*  

*Or with glue, lacquer, and cloth,*  
*All adorned as Buddha images—*  
*All such persons as these,*  
*Have already become Buddhas.*  

*Painted and rendered as Buddha images,*  
*Adorned with the hundred-fold blessings—*  
*Whether one creates or directs others to create—*  
*All have already become Buddhas.*  

*Even to the extent that children in play,*  
*With grass, wood, or a brush,*  
*Or with their fingernails or teeth,*  
*Paint Buddha images—*  

*All such persons as these,*  
*Gradually accumulate blessed merit,*  
*Complete the heart of great compassion,*  
*And have already become Buddhas.*  

*Transforming only Bodhisattvas,*  
*Liberating countless sentient beings.*  
*If a person, in stūpas or temples,*  
*With precious images and painted ones,*  

*Makes offerings with flowers, incense, banners, and canopies,*  
*With a heart of reverent respect.*  
*Or causes music to be made,*  
*Striking drums and blowing horns and conches,*  

*Flutes, lyres, and harps,*  
*Lutes and cymbals, and brass bells—*  
*All such marvelous sounds,*  
*Are employed in making offerings.*  

*Or with a joyful heart,*  
*One sings hymns praising the Buddha's virtue—*  
*To the extent of even one small utterance,*  
*All have already become Buddhas.*  

*If a person with distracted mind,*  
*Even offers a single flower,*  
*To a painted Buddha image,*  
*They shall gradually see countless Buddhas.*  

*Or if someone bows,*  
*Or merely joins their palms,*  
*Lifts a single hand,*  
*Or even slightly bows the head,*  

*In making offerings to an image—*  
*They shall gradually perceive countless Buddhas.*  
*And will themselves attain the supreme path,*  
*Widely liberating countless sentient beings,*  

*Entering the unconditioned parinirvāṇa,*  
*Like fire extinguished when fuel is exhausted.*  
*If a person with scattered mind,*  
*Enters into a stūpa or temple,*  

*And utters a single "Homage to the Buddha,"*  
*All have already become Buddhas.*  
*Regarding past Buddhas,*  
*Both in their living presence and after their passing,*  

*If any being hears this dharma,*  
*All have already become Buddhas.*  
*The future World-Honored Ones,*  
*Shall be boundless in number,*  

*All such Tathāgatas,*  
*Shall also teach the dharma through skillful means.*  
*All the Tathāgatas,*  
*Through immeasurable skillful means-power,*  

*Liberate all sentient beings,*  
*Causing them to enter the Buddha's unconditioned wisdom.*  
*If there is one who hears the dharma,*  
*There is none who shall not become a Buddha.*  

*All the Buddhas' original vows,*  
*Are that the path I have practiced,*  
*I universally desire all sentient beings,*  
*To attain this path equally.*  

*The future World-Honored Ones,*  
*Though teaching hundreds of billions,*  
*Of countless dharma-teachings,*  
*In reality constitute but one vehicle.*  

*All the Buddhas, the two-footed honored ones,*  
*Know that dharma is ever without nature,*  
*Buddha-seed arises from conditions—*  
*Therefore they teach the one vehicle.*[^33]  

---

## Conclusion: The Truth of the Teaching

"This dharma dwells in the dharmic position; the characteristics of the world ever remain constant. Upon the seat of enlightenment, having understood this, the guide teaches it through skillful means.[^34]

"The beings honored by heaven and people, the present Buddhas in the ten directions, numbering like Ganges' sands, appearing in the world in order to bring peace to sentient beings, all teach this same dharma.

"Understanding supreme quiescence through skillful means-power, although they show various paths, in reality this is the Buddha Vehicle. Understanding sentient beings' practices, the deep desires of their hearts, their past habits and karmic deeds, the desires and exertions of their faculties, dull and keen—through various causes, conditions, parables, and verbal expressions, they teach adaptively according to circumstances.

"I too am now in this manner, bringing peace to sentient beings through infinite dharma-teachings, revealing the Buddha path.

"I employ the power of wisdom to know sentient beings' natures and desires, teaching the dharmas through skillful means such that all attain joy.

"Śāriputra, with the Buddha-eye I observe the six-path sentient beings—poor, without virtue and good fortune, entering the dangerous path of birth and death, their suffering continuously unceasing, deeply attached to the five desires, like oxen attached to their own tails, blinded by greed and affection, unseeing.

"They do not seek the power of the great Mahāsattva Buddha, nor seek the dharma that cuts suffering; they deeply enter perverted views, and with suffering seek to transcend suffering.

"For the sake of such sentient beings, I generate the mind of great compassion.

"When I initially sat upon the seat of enlightenment, observing the trees and practicing circumambulation, within twenty-one days, I contemplated this matter: The wisdom I have obtained is subtle and supreme; sentient beings are dull-witted, bound by blindness and suffering—how can they be liberated?

"At that time, all the Brahmā kings, the devas of India, the Four Heavenly Kings, and the great Maheśvara devas, together with all celestial retinues numbering in the hundreds of thousands and millions, showed respect through palms joined in salutation, requesting me to turn the Dharma wheel.

"I then reflected within myself: If I merely praise the Buddha Vehicle, sentient beings submerged in suffering will be unable to generate faith in this dharma. If they reject the dharma due to faithlessness, they will fall into the three evil paths. It would be better for me not to teach the dharma and to quickly enter parinirvāṇa.[^35]

"Then I recalled the skillful means-power of the Buddhas of the past. Now, the path I have obtained should also employ teaching of the three vehicles.

"While contemplating this matter, all the Buddhas of the ten directions appeared to me and spoke with voices of Brahmā, comforting me: 'Well done, Śākyamuni, supreme guide! We have also obtained this dharma. In accordance with the conduct of all the Buddhas, we employ skillful means-power.

"'We too have all attained the supreme, most wonderful dharma. In order to teach all sentient beings, we make distinctions among the three vehicles. Although we teach the three vehicles, it is only to guide Bodhisattvas.

"'Those of limited wisdom delight in small dharmas and do not generate faith that they shall become Buddhas. This is why we employ skillful means, distinguishing the results.

"'Although we teach the three vehicles, in reality we guide all toward the Bodhisattva path.'

"Śāriputra, upon hearing the words of these Buddha-sages, and recalling their words, I rejoiced and called out, 'Homage to the Buddhas!' and contemplated this matter further.[^36]

"I realized that the appearance of Buddhas in the world is to teach Buddha-wisdom. And now is precisely the time to teach this dharma.[^37]

"Śāriputra, you should know: Those of dull faculties, those of limited wisdom, those seized by attachment and arrogant conceit, are unable to generate faith in this dharma.

"Now I am glad and without fear regarding all Bodhisattvas. I shall forthwith abandon skillful means and teach only the supreme path.

"The Bodhisattvas, having heard this dharma, will have their nets of doubt completely severed. The twelve hundred Arhats shall all become Buddhas.[^38]

"Just as all the Buddhas of the three times teach the dharma through such methods, I now likewise teach the undifferentiated dharma.

"The appearance of Buddhas in the world is extraordinarily rare, and the chance to encounter them is difficult; even if one encounters them, to hear this dharma is rare; and the capacity to listen to this dharma is rare indeed.

"It is comparable to the udumbara flower, which all love and esteem, which devas and humans regard as rare, appearing only at certain times.

"One who hears this dharma with joyful praise, even to the extent of uttering a single word, has already made offerings to all the Buddhas of the three times. Such a person is extremely rare, surpassing even the udumbara flower.

"You should harbor no doubt! I am the Dharma King of all dharmas,[^39] and I hereby universally announce to the great assembly: It is through the One Buddha Vehicle alone that I teach the Bodhisattvas. There are no voice-hearer disciples.

"You, Śāriputra, together with all voice-hearers and Bodhisattvas, should understand that this wonderful dharma is the secret treasury of all the Buddhas.[^40]

"Because the five corruptions exist in this evil world, sentient beings delight in and cling to various desires. Such beings will never seek the Buddha path. When those evil-natured humans of the future age hear the Buddha teaching the One Vehicle, confused and faithless, they will reject the dharma and fall into the evil paths.[^41]

"But for those possessing shame and purity, seeking the Buddha path, I shall extensively praise the One Vehicle path.

"Śāriputra, you should know: The dharma of all Buddhas is thus—through immeasurable skillful means, teaching dharmas according to circumstances. Those who have not studied and learned cannot fully comprehend this.[^42]

"Now that you have already understood the Buddhas' nature and the matter of teaching through skillful means, without any further doubts, your hearts generate great joy, and you shall surely become Buddhas."

---

## SCHOLARLY APPARATUS

### Footnote Commentary

1. **"Emerged from meditation with composed serenity"**: The phrase (anshang) suggests calm, measured, and deliberate movement, indicating that the Buddha's emergence from deep meditative absorption occurs with perfect composure. This physical embodiment of tranquility establishes the tone for the profound teachings to follow.

2. **"The Buddha's accomplishment of unprecedented teachings"**: The phrase "unprecedented" is repeated throughout this chapter and suggests that the Buddha's realization transcends all previous understanding and comprehension. This emphasizes the revolutionary nature of the Lotus Sutra's core message.

3. **"Transcend all forms of attachment and clinging"**: The Sanskrit term taṇhā (craving/clinging) is fundamental to Buddhist psychology. Attachment to fixed views, identities, and objects is understood as the root cause of suffering, and liberation requires transcending these forms of grasping.

 *Philosophical Parallel*: In existentialist philosophy, Sartre's concept of "bad faith" similarly refers to the human tendency to cling to fixed identities and predetermined essences rather than acknowledging radical freedom and responsibility.

4. **"Words gentle and soft that bring joy to the hearts of all beings"** (，): This phrase describes the skillful communication of ultimate truth through language that is psychologically attuned to listeners' capacities. It represents a profound understanding that the transmission of wisdom requires not merely intellectual accuracy but emotional and relational appropriateness.

5. **"The Tathāgata has accomplished infinite, boundless, unprecedented teachings"** (，): This statement establishes that Buddha-wisdom is not limited or partial but comprehensive and complete, transcending all previous conceptual frameworks and categories.

6. **"The supremely rare and extraordinary teaching"**: The use of "supremely rare" suggests that the teaching is not merely difficult but represents a categorical transcendence of ordinary understanding.

7. **"Ten dharmic characteristics"**: The ten characteristics listed—characteristics, nature, body, power, activity, cause, condition, effect, retribution, and beginning-and-end equality—constitute the Buddhist understanding of the fundamental structure of reality. These aren't merely philosophical abstractions but describe how any phenomenon (dharma) manifests across multiple dimensions of understanding.

 *Metaphysical Significance*: This passage suggests that ultimate reality is not some transcendent realm beyond the phenomenal world but rather a comprehensive understanding of how all phenomena actually are in their suchness (tathatā).

8. **"One meaning of liberation"**: The phrase indicates that the disciples understood a single type of liberation (Arhatship) but did not grasp the fuller implications for universal Buddhahood.

9. **"We do not understand toward what end this teaching aims"**: The assembly's confusion indicates a crisis of understanding—they believed they had achieved the ultimate goal but are now uncertain whether the Buddha is suggesting something beyond their attainment.

10. **Śāriputra's respectful inquiry**: Śāriputra, foremost among the Buddha's disciples in wisdom, serves as the representative questioner for the entire assembly. His willingness to ask despite his own great learning establishes the appropriate epistemic humility for receiving the Buddha's teaching.

11. **"The entire world of devas and humans would be astonished"** (，): The Buddha's hesitation suggests that the teaching is so revolutionary that it threatens the entire cosmological and spiritual worldview that has been established.

 *Hermeneutical Note*: This hesitation is a rhetorical device that creates narrative tension and emphasizes the radical nature of what is about to be taught.

12. **"Their faculties are keen and sharp, their wisdom is clear and lucid"** (，): These adjectives describe practitioners who have developed their spiritual capacity through long training and thus are capable of receiving teachings that would confound those of lesser attainment.

13. **"Monks seized by arrogant conceit will fall into a great abyss"** (、): The concept of "arrogant conceit" refers to the false presumption of attainment—claiming realization one has not actually achieved. This is presented as a serious spiritual danger.

14. **"Ones comparable to yourself... across countless ages"** (、，): This phrase indicates that the assembly includes not merely contemporary practitioners but those who have been cultivated by the Buddha across countless lifetimes, suggesting a deeply rooted spiritual readiness.

15. **"You have made your earnest request three times"**: The threefold request (a classical rhetorical pattern in Buddhist texts) establishes sufficient earnestness to justify the Buddha's consent. In Buddhist narrative convention, three requests constitute a binding obligation to respond.

16. **"Five thousand departed"** (，，): This departure is highly significant—those whose karmic roots are not yet sufficiently developed cannot remain to hear the supreme teaching. The number five thousand may be symbolic rather than literal.

17. **"Deep karmic roots of transgression and arrogant conceit"** (，): The Buddhist understanding of karma (Sanskrit: karma; Chinese:, yè) refers to actions and their inevitable consequences. Those with deep roots of transgression (negative karma) cannot generate the faith necessary to receive the supreme teaching.

18. **"Entirely purified, without superfluous branches—consisting only of authentic fruit"** (，): This metaphor suggests that what remains is the essential core of authentic practitioners, with all extraneous elements removed. The contrast between "branches" (superficial growth) and "fruit" (true attainment) represents quality over quantity.

19. **"The udumbara flower, which blooms but once in a very long age"** (，): In Buddhist legend, the udumbara (Sanskrit: udumbara) flower is an extraordinarily rare celestial blossom that blooms only once in three thousand years. Its extreme rarity makes it the perfect metaphor for the infrequency of Buddha-teaching and human capacity to receive it.

 *Comparative Significance*: This metaphor appears in multiple Buddhist texts and represents a universal principle—that the opportunity to encounter true teaching is exceedingly rare.

20. **"Four aspects of Buddha-perception"** (、、、): These four aspects represent progressive stages: (1) opening the gateway to Buddha-wisdom, (2) showing or revealing Buddha-wisdom, (3) enabling awakening to Buddha-wisdom, and (4) guiding entry into the path of Buddha-wisdom. Together they suggest a comprehensive transformation of consciousness.

 *Epistemological Significance*: These four phrases describe the complete pedagogical process through which the Buddha transforms the consciousness of sentient beings from ignorance to enlightenment.

21. **"The One Great Cause"**: This phrase is central to the Lotus Sutra's metaphysics. The "one great cause" (singular, unified) for which all Buddhas appear in the world is to enable all sentient beings to realize Buddha-wisdom. All other teachings and paths are understood as manifestations of this one fundamental purpose.

 *Metaphysical Principle*: This represents the Lotus Sutra's assertion of ultimate unity—beneath apparent multiplicity of teachings and paths lies a singular, unified purpose and reality.

22. **"Neither two nor three"** (，、): This statement directly denies the validity of the three vehicles as ultimate reality. While the Buddha teaches through the framework of three vehicles (adapted to different capacities), ultimate truth is singular and unified.

23. **"All the Buddhas of the ten directions teach the dharma in this very manner"** (，): This universal principle establishes that the teaching of the One Vehicle is not unique to Śākyamuni but characteristic of all Buddhas throughout time and space. It is a cosmic principle rather than a particular doctrine.

24. **"The wisdom-knowing-all-aspects"**: This term represents the comprehensive, all-knowing wisdom of Buddhahood—consciousness that simultaneously perceives all phenomena in all their aspects and relationships. It is the ultimate goal of Buddhist practice.

 *Systems Theory Parallel*: This concept might be understood as analogous to systems-thinking awareness that perceives complex wholes while comprehending individual components and their interrelationships.

25. **"My purpose is to realize the One Buddha Vehicle and the wisdom-knowing-all-aspects"** (、): This explicit statement of the Buddha's motivation unifies the various teachings around a single purpose—to guide all beings to comprehensive wisdom and enlightenment.

26. **"Five corruptions and turbidities"**: These are: (1) corruption of the age—the world's degeneration, (2) corruption of afflictions—the intensification of mental defilements, (3) corruption of sentient beings—the degradation of living beings' capacities, (4) corruption of views—the proliferation of perverted understandings, (5) corruption of lifespan—the shortening of human life. These represent the conditions that make direct teaching of Buddha-wisdom difficult and necessitate skillful means.

 *Cosmological Significance*: The concept of five corruptions places the Lotus Sutra in a particular historical moment—the age of dharma-decline (mappo in Japanese Buddhism)—when beings are less capable of understanding the full teaching and thus require graduated instruction.

27. **"The Buddhas... within the One Buddha Vehicle, make distinctions and teach the three vehicles"** (，、): This is the central formulation of skillful means—the three vehicles are not separate ultimate realities but distinctions made for pedagogical purposes within the unified framework of the One Buddha Vehicle. This represents a radical reconceptualization of Buddhist teaching hierarchy.

 *Philosophical Innovation*: This teaching revolutionizes Buddhist thought by asserting that apparent contradictions in Buddhist doctrine (the three vehicles) are not genuinely contradictory but represent different presentations of a unified truth.

28. **"Not disciples of the Buddha, not Arhats, and not Pratyekabuddhas"** (，，): This statement has profound implications—one who hears the teaching of universal Buddha-nature but refuses to believe it disqualifies oneself from authentic discipleship, regardless of one's formal status or past attainments.

29. **"If he does not believe in this dharma, such a situation is impossible"** (，): This assertion establishes a logical principle—if someone has truly realized Arhatship (direct realization of the nature of conditioned existence), they must necessarily accept the teaching of universal Buddha-nature because it follows from understanding dependent origination.

30. **"There are no other vehicles—the Buddha Vehicle alone exists"** (，): This direct statement denies the ultimate validity of the three-vehicle framework and asserts the singular ultimacy of the Buddha Vehicle. This represents the Lotus Sutra's most radical metaphysical claim.

31. **"The seal of the real nature of reality"**: The "seal" (mudra) represents an official stamp of authenticity. The "real nature of reality" refers to the ultimate, unchanging nature of all phenomena as understood in Buddhist philosophy.

32. **"Further reveal the supreme reality"**: The phrase "supreme reality" refers to the ultimate, unconditioned dimension beyond ordinary conceptual understanding. All skillful means ultimately serve to reveal this supreme reality.

33. **"Buddha-seed arises from conditions—Therefore they teach the one vehicle"** (，): This condensed philosophical statement suggests that Buddhahood itself arises through dependent origination (conditioned arising)—it is not a fixed, transcendent state but emerges through the causes and conditions of practice and receiving the teaching. Therefore, all beings who encounter the conditions (hearing the teaching) possess the capacity for Buddhahood.

 *Metaphysical Significance*: This represents a profound synthesis—the principle of dependent origination (the most fundamental Buddhist principle) is applied to understand Buddhahood itself as arising through conditions rather than being a transcendent, unchanging state.

34. **"This dharma dwells in the dharmic position; the characteristics of the world ever remain constant"** (，): This statement suggests that the dharma described is not something new or artificially constructed but rather the eternal, unchanging nature of reality itself. The "dharmic position" refers to reality's fundamental structure.

35. **"Enter parinirvāṇa"**: The Buddha's contemplation of whether to teach or immediately enter parinirvāṇa establishes the profound difficulty of the teaching task. The Buddha recognizes that teaching the one vehicle risks rejection and thus descent into evil paths, whereas entering parinirvāṇa would spare beings from this danger.

 *Narrative Significance*: This inner dialogue establishes that the Buddha's ultimate choice to teach represents a compassionate sacrifice—accepting the risk that some will reject and thereby fall into negative karmic consequences, in order that others might receive the supreme teaching.

36. **"Words of the Buddha-sages"**: The metaphor of "lion" (lion-roar, simhanada) refers to the Buddha's fearless proclamation of truth. The lion's roar is traditionally understood as symbolic of the Buddha's teachings that cut through all opposing views.

37. **"Now is precisely the time to teach this dharma"**: This establishes a principle of temporal appropriateness—teachings must be given at the right moment in history and in the development of individual practitioners' consciousness. Premature or delayed teaching lacks efficacy.

38. **"The twelve hundred Arhats shall all become Buddhas"** (、): This prophecy, extended to all present disciples, symbolizes the universal capacity for Buddhahood and represents the complete overturning of previous hierarchies that placed Arhatship as the ultimate goal for disciples.

39. **"I am the Dharma King of all dharmas"**: This title asserts the Buddha's supreme authority and mastery over all teachings and dharmas, establishing him as the source and arbiter of all valid understanding.

40. **"The secret treasury of all the Buddhas"**: The teaching of the One Vehicle is described as the "secret treasury" —a precious, hidden teaching that has been reserved and protected by Buddhas throughout history, now revealed to fortunate practitioners.

 *Hermeneutical Significance*: The concept of "secret" (in Buddhist hermeneutics) does not mean intentionally hidden from some while revealed to others, but rather refers to teachings that are accessible only to those with sufficient spiritual capacity to comprehend them.

41. **"Evil-natured humans of the future age"**: This reference to future ages suggests that the Lotus Sutra anticipates a time of spiritual degeneration when beings will be less capable of receiving the supreme teaching. This reflects the Buddhist historical periodization of mappō (the latter age of dharma decline).

42. **"Those who have not studied and learned cannot fully comprehend this"** (，): This final statement establishes that understanding the Lotus Sutra's teaching on skillful means requires prior cultivation—study, practice, and training in Buddhist philosophy and meditation.

---

### Philosophical Implications
### Key Concepts Introduced in Chapter 2:

### Skilful Means (Upaya)
The central concept of Chapter 2—that all Buddhist teachings, even apparently contradictory ones, are expressions of a single unified purpose. The Buddha adapts teachings to different capacities and circumstances while aiming at a singular ultimate realization.

**Philosophical Significance**: This concept resolves apparent contradictions in Buddhist doctrine by asserting that differences in teaching reflect differences in method (upaya) rather than differences in truth. All paths lead to the same destination, though the routes vary.

**Contemporary Parallel**: In modern systems thinking, skillful means parallels the principle that complex systems can be understood through multiple valid frameworks and descriptions, each appropriate to particular contexts and perspectives.
### The One Buddha Vehicle
While the Buddha teaches through the framework of three vehicles (Śrāvaka, Pratyekabuddha, Bodhisattva), ultimate reality recognizes only the One Buddha Vehicle—the universal path to complete Buddhahood.

**Metaphysical Implication**: This represents a fundamental assertion about the nature of ultimate reality as unified and singular, beneath apparent multiplicity.
### Universal Buddha-nature
Chapter 2 reaffirms and develops the principle introduced in Chapter 1: all sentient beings without exception possess Buddha-nature and thus possess the intrinsic capacity to become Buddhas.
### The Five Corruptions
These represent the historical moment and conditions that necessitate skillful means—a world of spiritual degeneration in which beings cannot directly comprehend Buddha-wisdom and thus require graduated instruction.
### The Transcendence of Language and Concept
The repeated assertion that Buddha-wisdom "cannot be expressed in words" and "transcends thinking and discrimination" points to a non-dualistic understanding that reality surpasses conceptual understanding. Yet paradoxically, the Buddha employs infinite skillful means of language, metaphor, and parable to communicate this truth.

### Apparatus Summary

**Key Philosophical Concepts**: Skillful means (upaya), One Buddha Vehicle, Universal Buddha-nature, Five Corruptions, Transcendence of language and concept

**Cross-Chapter References**:
- **Chapter 1**: Establishes the principle of universal Buddha-nature through the parable of Burning Lamps Buddha
- **Chapter 3 (Parables)**: Provides extensive parables illustrating the principle of skillful means
- **Chapter 4 (Faith and Understanding)**: Śāriputra and others fully accept the teaching announced in Chapter 2
- **Chapters 5-7**: Develop the implications through parables of medicinal herbs, prophecies, and the phantom city
- **Chapter 16 (Lifespan of the Tathāgata)**: Extends the principle to cosmology, revealing the Buddha's eternal nature

---

*End of Chapter 2*
---

# Chapter 3: Parables

## Śāriputra's Joy and Understanding

At that time, Śāriputra, overflowing with joy and delight, immediately rose from his seat, pressed his palms together in salutation, gazed upon the Buddha's face with reverent attention, and addressed the Buddha:

"Now, having heard this dharma-sound from the World-Honored One, my heart overflows with joy and I have experienced what has never been experienced before. Why is this?

"In ancient times, when I heard from the Buddha such a teaching as this, I saw that the various Bodhisattvas received prophecies of Buddhahood, yet I and my companions were not included in these events. I deeply sorrowed within myself, feeling that I had lost the infinite wisdom of the Tathāgata.[^1]

"World-Honored One, I have often dwelt alone in mountain forests and beneath trees, whether sitting or walking, constantly entertaining this thought: 'We have all entered into the dharma-nature equally—why then does the Tathāgata appear to guide us through the small vehicle?' This fault lies with us, not with the World-Honored One. Why is this?

"Had we expected the teaching of that which would enable us to achieve anuttara-samyak-saṃbodhi, we would surely have been liberated through the great vehicle. However, we did not understand the skillful means of adaptive teaching according to circumstances. Upon first hearing the Buddha's teaching, we encountered it and immediately generated faith, contemplated it, and claimed realization.

"World-Honored One, since ancient times I have continuously and throughout day and night reproached myself. Yet now, having heard from the Buddha teachings never before heard, unprecedented dharmas, I have severed all doubt and regret. My body and mind are at peace, my heart is utterly tranquil. Today I truly understand that I am a Buddha-child, born from the Buddha's mouth, born from the dharma, and possessed of the Buddha-dharma inheritance."[^2]

---

## Śāriputra's Verse of Joy

Then Śāriputra, wishing to restate this meaning, spoke in verse:

*Having heard this dharma-sound,*  
*I have obtained what was never before experienced,*  
*My heart overflows with great joy,*  
*And all my nets of doubt have been severed.*  

*In the past, receiving the Buddha's teaching,*  
*I did not lose the great vehicle,*  
*The Buddha's sound is supremely rare,*  
*Able to remove all sentient beings' afflictions.*  

*I have already exhausted all outflows,*  
*And this hearing has removed my distress.*  
*I dwelt in valleys*  
*Or beneath forest trees,*  

*Whether sitting or walking in meditation,*  
*I constantly contemplated this matter,*  
*Grieving deeply and reproaching myself:*  
*How could I deceive myself?*  

*We too are Buddha-children,*  
*Having entered into the unconditioned dharma together,*  
*Yet I have been unable in the future,*  
*To expound the supreme path.*  

*The thirty-two marks of gold,*  
*The ten powers and all liberations,*  
*Shared within one dharma,*  
*Yet I did not obtain this.*  

*The eighty wonderful characteristics,*  
*The eighteen dharmas unique to Buddhas,*  
*And such meritorious qualities as these,*  
*I have altogether lost.*  

*During my solitary practice, walking,*  
*I saw the Buddha in the great assembly,*  
*His renown filling the ten directions,*  
*Bringing immeasurable benefit to all beings.*  

*I contemplated how I had lost this benefit,*  
*And knew that I had deceived myself.*  
*Constantly throughout day and night,*  
*I contemplated this matter,*  

*Wishing to question the World-Honored One,*  
*Whether I had lost or not lost this,*  
*I constantly saw the World-Honored One,*  
*Praising the various Bodhisattvas.*  

*Because of this, throughout day and night,*  
*I deliberated upon this matter.*  
*Now, hearing the Buddha's voice,*  
*Teaching the dharma according to circumstances,*  

*The unconditioned, inconceivable dharma,*  
*That brings all beings to enlightenment.*  
*I was originally attached to perverted views,*  
*A teacher to all the Brahmin ascetics.*  

*The World-Honored One knew my mind,*  
*And uprooted my perverted views by teaching nirvāṇa.*  
*I have completely eliminated perverted views,*  
*And realized the dharma of emptiness.*  

*At that time, my mind believed,*  
*That I had attained extinction.*  
*Yet now I have suddenly awakened,*  
*That this is not true extinction.*  

*When I obtain Buddhahood,*  
*Possessing the thirty-two marks,*  
*The devas, humans, yakshas,*  
*And dragon-beings showing reverence,*  

*Only then can it be truly said,*  
*That extinction has been completed without remainder.*  
*The Buddha, in the great assembly,*  
*Has taught that I shall become a Buddha.*  

*Having heard such dharma-sound,*  
*All my doubt and regret have been severed.*  
*When I first heard what the Buddha taught,*  
*My heart harbored great astonishment and doubt,*  

*"Could this be the work of Māra,*  
*Creating a false Buddha, disturbing my mind?"*  
*The Buddha, through various causes and conditions,*  
*Parables and skillfully chosen words, spoke,*  

*And my heart became calm as the ocean,*  
*My nets of doubt were severed upon hearing.*  
*The Buddha spoke of past world-ages,*  
*Countless Buddhas who had passed away,*  

*Who dwell within skillful means,*  
*And all taught this very dharma.*  
*The Buddhas of the present and future,*  
*Are boundless in number,*  

*And also through skillful means,*  
*Expound this dharma likewise.*  
*Just as the World-Honored One of today,*  
*From birth and renunciation of family life,*  

*Attained the path and turned the dharma-wheel,*  
*Also teaching through skillful means.*  
*The World-Honored One speaks the true path—*  
*Māra has no such accomplishment.*  

*Therefore, I know with certainty,*  
*That this is not the work of Māra.*  
*I had fallen into nets of doubt,*  
*Thinking it was Māra's doing.*  

*Upon hearing the Buddha's gentle and soft voice,*  
*Profoundly mysterious and supremely subtle,*  
*Expounding the pure dharma,*  
*My heart was filled with great joy.*  

*Doubt and regret have forever been severed,*  
*And I dwell firmly in true wisdom.*  
*I shall certainly become a Buddha,*  
*Honored by devas and humans,*  

*Turn the supreme dharma-wheel,*  
*And teach the various Bodhisattvas.*  

---

## The Buddha's Prophecy for Śāriputra

Then the Buddha addressed Śāriputra: "Now I speak before the assembly of devas, humans, monks, and Brahmins: I previously, for immeasurable billions of eons, in the presence of twenty billion Buddhas, for the sake of achieving the supreme path, continually taught and transformed you. You also, for countless nights, followed me and received my teaching.

"Because I guided you through skillful means, you were born within my teaching. Śāriputra, in the past I taught you to aspire to the Buddha path. You have now forgotten this, and thus you presume you have already attained extinction. Now I wish to cause you to recollect your original vow and the path you cultivated. For this reason, for all the Śrāvakas I teach this great-vehicle sutra, called the *Sutra of the Wonderful Dharma Lotus Flower*, the teaching for Bodhisattvas, protected and remembered by all the Buddhas."[^3]

---

## Śāriputra's Future Buddhahood

"Śāriputra, in the future world, after passing through immeasurable, boundless, inconceivable eons, having made offerings to countless billions of Buddhas, upheld the correct dharma, perfected the bodhisattva path, you shall become a Buddha with the title Tathāgata of Radiant Flower, Worthy of Offerings, Possessor of Perfect Knowledge, Possessor of Perfect Conduct, Well-Gone, Knower of the World, Unsurpassed Guide, Trainer of Beings, Teacher of Devas and Humans, Buddha, World-Honored One.

"The land will be named Pure Radiance, its earth level and even, supremely pure and beautifully adorned, secure and prosperous, with abundance of devas and humans. Its ground shall be made of lapis lazuli, with eight crossways adorned with golden boundaries. Throughout the realm shall grow seven-jeweled trees, eternally bearing flowers and fruit. The Tathāgata of Radiant Flower shall also teach the three vehicles to sentient beings."[^4]

---

## The Golden Age of Radiant Flower Buddha

"Śāriputra, at the time of this Buddha's emergence, although it will not be an evil age, because of his original vow, he will teach the three-vehicle dharma. The eon shall be named 'Great Jeweled Adornment.' Why is it called 'Great Jeweled Adornment'? Because the beings considered the great jewels of that realm are the Bodhisattvas themselves.[^5]

"Those Bodhisattvas shall be boundless, immeasurable, and inconceivable—beyond the capacity of calculation, parable, or comparison. Only the Buddha's wisdom has the capacity to know them. When they desire to go about, precious flowers manifest beneath their feet.

"These Bodhisattvas are not newly-aspiring ones but have long planted roots of virtue and cultivated the Brahmin conduct purely in the presence of countless billions of Buddhas. They are continuously praised by all Buddhas, constantly cultivate Buddha-wisdom, possess great supernatural powers, skillfully understand the gateways to all dharmas, are direct and without deception, and have fixed, unwavering intentions. Such Bodhisattvas fill that Buddha-realm entirely."[^6]

---

## THE PARABLE OF THE BURNING HOUSE

## The Setting and the Fire

"Śāriputra, if in a country, city, or village there is a great householder—elderly, with wealth without measure, possessing much land, houses, servants, and retainers. His house is vast with only a single gate. Many people—one hundred, two hundred, up to five hundred—dwell within it. The buildings are decayed and deteriorated, the walls cracked and crumbling, the pillars at their foundations rotting and corrupting, the roof beams tilting dangerously. All around simultaneously, fire suddenly erupts, burning the entire dwelling.[^7]

"The householder's children—ten, twenty, or as many as thirty—are within this house. The householder sees the great fire rising from all four sides, is immediately seized with tremendous alarm, and thinks within himself:

"'Although I am able to escape safely from this burning gate, my children, being within the burning house, are absorbed in play and amusement, unaware and unknowing, neither startled nor fearful. The fire approaches their bodies, the pain is acute, yet their minds harbor no loathing, nor do they harbor any thought of escaping.'"[^8]

---

## The Father's Dilemma and First Attempt

"Śāriputra, the householder then thinks: 'My body possesses strength and skill. I should use my garments or a cloth to pull the children out of the house.' Then again he thinks: 'This house has only one gate, and it is narrow and small. The children are very young, without understanding or awareness, clinging to their playing places. When they come out, they might fall down or be burned by the fire. I must describe to them the frightening nature of this situation—this house is already burning, and it is fitting that you quickly exit, so that the fire does not burn and harm you.'

"Having this thought, he carefully tells all his children: 'You must quickly exit!' Though the father is compassionate and entices them with gentle words, the children, absorbed in play, do not believe him, are neither startled nor afraid, and have no intention of exiting. They do not know what fire is, what the house is, what being lost means, but only run about in play to the east and west, glancing at their father."[^9]

---

## The Father's Skilful Means

"At that time the householder immediately thinks: 'This house is greatly burning in flames. If I and my children do not exit in time, we shall all be burned. Now I must establish skillful means to enable my children to escape this danger.'

"The father knows that each of his children previously harbored various preferences for rare and wonderful playthings and objects, which their feelings naturally delighted in. He tells them:

"'The playthings and precious objects that delight you are rare and difficult to obtain. If you do not take them, you will surely regret it later. There are here various kinds of sheep-carts, deer-carts, and ox-carts, now outside the gate, which you can use for play. Come quickly out of this burning house, and according to your wishes, I shall give you each one of these carts.'

"At that moment, the children, hearing their father speak of these rare playthings suited to their desires, each became eager and sharp in mind, competing with one another and pushing forward in rivalry, hastening to run out of the burning house.[^10]

"At this time the householder, seeing his children safely escape, all seated in the open space at the four crossroads, unobstructed and secure, his heart at peace and overflowing with joy, thinks to himself:

"Then his children each address their father: 'Father, the playthings and precious objects you previously promised us—the sheep-carts, deer-carts, and ox-carts—we wish you to give them to us now.'"[^11]

---

## The Gift of the Great Carriage

"Śāriputra, at that time the householder bestows upon each child one great carriage. That carriage is tall and spacious, adorned and decorated with multitudinous precious materials, surrounded by railings, with bells suspended from all four sides. From its top hangs a jeweled canopy, also decorated and adorned with rare and various treasures. Jeweled ropes interweave, and precious flower-garlands hang down. Soft fabrics are thickly spread within, and cushioned with crimson pillows are arranged for reclining.

"It is drawn by white oxen, with lustrous and pure skin, beautiful in form, possessing great strength and power, steady and even in gait, swift as wind. Many servants accompany and protect it. Why is this?[^12]

"This great householder possesses wealth without limit, and all his various treasuries are completely full and overflowing. Yet he thinks:

"'My wealth is without end—it is not fitting that I give my children inferior small carts. All these youths are my sons, and I love them without partiality. I possess such seven-jeweled great carts without measure, and it is fitting that I give them with equal mind to each, without favoritism. Why is this? Even if I were to distribute these possessions throughout an entire realm, there would still be no exhaustion—how much more so for my sons?'

"At this time each of the children, riding the great carriage, experienced what they had never before experienced, and what they had not originally expected."[^13]

---

## The Buddha's Application

"Śāriputra, what do you think of this matter? Is the householder's giving of these precious great carts to the children deceptive in any way?"[^14]

Śāriputra replied: "No, World-Honored One. The householder merely caused his children to escape the fire-danger and preserve their lives—this is not deceptive. Why? Because preserving their bodies and lives, they have already obtained the playthings they desired, much more so through skillful means to rescue them from the burning house. World-Honored One, even if the householder had not given them even the smallest single cart, it would still not be deceptive. Why? Because the householder previously formed this intention: 'I shall employ skillful means to enable my children to escape.' By this cause and condition, there is no deception. How much more so when the householder, knowing his wealth is immeasurable, wishes to benefit his children and gives them equally great carts."[^15]

---

## The Central Teaching: The Burning House and the Three Worlds

Then the Buddha said to Śāriputra: "Excellent! Excellent! As you have spoken. Śāriputra, the Tathāgata is likewise the father of all the world.[^16]

"The Tathāgata has completely exhausted all fears, afflictions, distress, sorrow, and ignorance and darkness—having realized infinite wisdom and knowledge, fearlessness, great supernatural power, and wisdom-power. The Tathāgata has perfected the perfection of skillful means and wisdom, great compassion and great loving-kindness, never ceasing in endeavor, constantly seeking to benefit all beings.[^17]

"The Tathāgata, being born within the three-world decaying house, in order to liberate sentient beings from birth, old age, sickness, death, sorrow, affliction, pain, ignorance, and darkness—from the fire of the three poisons (greed, hatred, delusion)—teaches the dharma, enabling them to attain anuttara-samyak-saṃbodhi.[^18]

"The Tathāgata sees sentient beings burned by birth, old age, sickness, death, sorrow, affliction, and pain. Because of the five desires and material wealth, they undergo various sufferings. Because of their greedy attachment and pursuit, they experience present suffering, and in the future will experience the suffering of hell-beings, animals, and hungry ghosts. If born among devas and humans, they experience poverty, suffering, separation from loved ones, meeting with those they hate—all manner of such various sufferings.

"Sentient beings are submerged within these sufferings, yet take joy in their entertainments and games, unaware and unknowing, neither startled nor afraid, generating no loathing and seeking no liberation. In this three-world burning house, they run about to east and west. Though they encounter great suffering, they do not regard it as a calamity.

"Śāriputra, the Buddha, having seen this, forms this thought: 'I am the father of sentient beings—I should free them from their suffering and hardship, and grant them the joy of infinite, boundless Buddha-wisdom, enabling them to engage in play.'[^19]"

---

## The Buddha's Internal Deliberation

"Śāriputra, the Tathāgata again forms this thought: 'If I merely employ supernatural power and wisdom-power, abandoning skillful means, and for sentient beings praise the Tathāgata's wisdom, fearlessness, and powers, sentient beings would not thereby be liberated. Why? These sentient beings have not yet escaped birth, old age, sickness, death, and sorrow—they are burned by the three-world burning house. How could they comprehend Buddha-wisdom?'[^20]"

---

## The Buddha's Skilful Teaching of the Three Vehicles

"Śāriputra, as the householder, though possessed of physical strength and skill, did not employ it, but only through persistent skillful means labored to rescue his children from the burning house, and afterward gave them precious great carts—the Tathāgata is likewise:

"Although possessing power and fearlessness, the Tathāgata does not employ it. Rather, through wisdom and skillful means, within the three-world burning house, the Tathāgata liberates sentient beings, teaching the three vehicles—the Śrāvaka Vehicle, the Pratyekabuddha Vehicle, and the Buddha Vehicle—and speaks as follows:[^21]

"'You must not take pleasure in dwelling within the three-world burning house. Do not covet the coarse and inferior—forms, sounds, smells, tastes, and tactile sensations. If you grasp at them with craving and attachment, you will be burned. You must quickly emerge from the three worlds and attain the three vehicles—the Śrāvaka Vehicle, the Pratyekabuddha Vehicle, and the Buddha Vehicle. I give you my assurance of this matter; it shall never be false. You need only diligently cultivate mindfulness.'

"The Tathāgata, through these skillful means, encourages sentient beings forward, and again speaks: 'You should know that these three-vehicle dharmas are all praised by the sages, are free and unbound, with nothing to depend upon or seek. By means of these three vehicles, employing the unconditioned roots, powers, awakening-factors, path-factors, meditation, liberation, samadhi, and such, you shall entertain yourselves and obtain countless security, ease, and happiness.'"[^22]

---

## The Three Vehicles Explained

"Śāriputra, if there are sentient beings who possess inner wisdom-nature, who hear the dharma from the Buddha, the World-Honored One, generate faith and receive it, diligently cultivate, and speedily wish to escape the three worlds, seeking nirvāṇa for themselves—this is called the Śrāvaka Vehicle, like those children who sought the sheep-carts and exited the burning house.[^23]

"If there are sentient beings who hear the dharma from the Buddha, the World-Honored One, generate faith and receive it, diligently cultivate, seek the wisdom arising of itself, delight in solitary peace, and deeply understand the causes and conditions of all dharmas—this is called the Pratyekabuddha Vehicle, like those children who sought the deer-carts and exited the burning house.[^24]

"If there are sentient beings who hear the dharma from the Buddha, the World-Honored One, generate faith and receive it, diligently cultivate, seek the wisdom-knowing-all-aspects, Buddha-wisdom, wisdom-arising-of-itself, wisdom-without-teacher, the Tathāgata's wisdom and vision, the Buddha's power and fearlessness, who have compassion for and give ease to countless sentient beings, who benefit devas and humans, and liberate all beings—this is called the Great Vehicle. Bodhisattvas seek this vehicle, and therefore are called Mahāsattvas (Great Beings), like those children who sought the ox-carts and exited the burning house."[^25]

---

## The Ultimate Gift: The One Vehicle

"Śāriputra, as the householder, having seen his children safely escape from the burning house and arrive at the fearless place, knowing his wealth is without measure, grants to each child these great vehicles as equal gifts—the Tathāgata is likewise:

"Being the father of all sentient beings, when the Tathāgata sees countless billions of sentient beings, having escaped the suffering and fear of the three-world dangerous path through the Buddha-teaching gate, having attained the joy of nirvāṇa, the Tathāgata at that time forms this thought:

"'I possess infinite, boundless wisdom, fearlessness, and all the Buddha-dharma treasuries. All these sentient beings are my children. I shall equally grant them the great vehicle, and not allow any being to attain liberation alone.'

"All sentient beings are liberated by means of the Tathāgata's nirvāṇa. All these sentient beings who have escaped the three worlds are provided with the treasuries for entertainment of all the Buddhas—meditative absorptions, liberations, and such—all of which are of one characteristic, one type, praised by the sages, and capable of producing the purest, most supreme joy."[^26]

---

## The Resolution: No Deception in Skilful Means

"Śāriputra, as the householder initially employed three carts to entice his children, and subsequently gave them only the great carriage, with precious treasures and adornment, the most secure and supreme—yet that householder harbored no deception.

"The Tathāgata is likewise—there is no deception. The Tathāgata initially teaches the three vehicles to guide and transform sentient beings, and subsequently liberates all through the great vehicle alone. Why is this? Because the Tathāgata possesses infinite wisdom, fearlessness, and all dharma-treasuries, and is able to grant the great-vehicle dharma to all sentient beings—only they do not all have the capacity to receive it.[^27]

"Śāriputra, by means of this cause and condition, you should understand: The Buddhas, through the power of skillful means, within the One Buddha Vehicle, make distinctions and teach the three vehicles."[^28]

---

## The Complete Vision of the Buddha's Teaching

Then the Buddha, wishing to restate this meaning, spoke in verse elaborating the entire parable with vivid detail and concluding:

*The World-Honored One is like this—*  
*Honored among all sages,*  
*Father of all sentient beings.*  
*All are my children,*  

*Deeply clinging to worldly pleasures,*  
*Lacking wisdom.*  
*The three worlds are without peace,*  
*Like a burning house,*  

*Full of all manner of suffering,*  
*Extremely fearful.*  
*Eternally possessing birth, old age, sickness, death, and sorrow—*  
*Such fires continuously blaze.*  

*The Tathāgata has already escaped the three-world burning house,*  
*Dwelling in tranquility, in peaceful solitude.*  
*Now this three worlds is wholly mine,*  
*And all its sentient beings are my children.*  

*Yet this realm contains many sufferings,*  
*Only I alone am able to rescue and protect them.*  
*Though I teach continually, they do not believe and accept.*  
*Regarding worldly desires and attachments, they cling deeply.*  

*By means of these skillful means,*  
*I teach the three vehicles,*  
*Enabling sentient beings to understand the three-world suffering,*  
*Opening and revealing the path of transcendence.*  

*If these children's minds become determined,*  
*Perfecting the three clear-knowings and six supernatural powers,*  
*Some obtain the pratyekabuddha realization or become non-retrogressive Bodhisattvas.*  
*You, Śāriputra, I teach to sentient beings,*  

*Through this parable,*  
*The One Buddha Vehicle—*  
*If all of you can generate faith in this teaching,*  
*Then all shall certainly attain the Buddha path.*  

*This vehicle is subtle and wonderful,*  
*Pure and supreme,*  
*In all the worlds it has no superior—*  
*Praised and honored by all Buddhas and sentient beings,*  

*Worthy of reverence and worship.*  
*Possessing countless billions of supernatural powers, liberations,*  
*Concentrations, wisdom, and other Buddha-dharmas,*  
*This vehicle grants to all children,*  

*Day and night, throughout eons,*  
*Constant play and entertainment,*  
*Together with all Bodhisattvas and śrāvaka assemblies,*  
*Riding this precious vehicle,*  

*Directly reaching enlightenment.*  
*By this cause and condition,*  
*Seeking throughout the ten directions,*  
*No other vehicle exists,*  

*Except the Buddha's skillful means.*  
*Tell Śāriputra:*  
*You and all others are my children—*  
*I am your father.*  

*Throughout countless eons,*  
*You have all been burned by manifold sufferings.*  
*I have completely rescued and liberated you,*  
*Enabling you to escape the three worlds.*  

*Though I previously taught*  
*That you have attained extinction—*  
*Merely exhausting birth and death—*  
*In truth, you have not been extinguished.*  

*What now remains to be accomplished*  
*Is only Buddha-wisdom.*  
*If any Bodhisattva in this assembly,*  
*Can listen wholeheartedly to the true dharma of all Buddhas,*  

*All Buddhas, the World-Honored Ones,*  
*Though employing skillful means,*  
*Transform all sentient beings into Bodhisattvas.*  
*If there are those of limited wisdom,*  

*Deeply attached to love and desire,*  
*For such beings I teach the suffering-truth.*  
*Sentient beings' hearts delight,*  
*Obtaining what has never been obtained,*  

*The Buddha's teaching of the suffering-truth—*  
*Truly without deception.*  
*If there are sentient beings*  
*Who do not know suffering's origin,*  

*Deeply attached to suffering's cause,*  
*Unable to abandon it for even a moment—*  
*For such beings, I skillfully teach the path.*  
*The cause of all suffering is greed and desire;*  

*If greed and desire are eliminated,*  
*There is nowhere for them to depend—*  
*Destroying all suffering completely,*  
*This is named the third truth.*  

*In order to extinguish the truth of cessation,*  
*One practices the path,*  
*Severing all suffering's bonds—*  
*This is called obtaining liberation.*  

---

## WARNINGS REGARDING THE LOTUS SUTRA'S TRANSMISSION

"Śāriputra, you should not proclaim this dharma-seal of mine carelessly in places you travel. If there is one who hears, generates joy, and accepts it respectfully, understand that such a person shall attain non-retrogression. If any being generates faith and accepts this sutra-dharma, understand that such a person has already seen the Buddhas of the past, shown them respect and reverence, and also heard this dharma.

"Whoever can generate faith in what you teach shall see me, shall see you, and shall see the bhikṣu assembly and all the Bodhisattvas. This Sutra of the Wonderful Dharma is for those of profound wisdom. The shallow-minded, upon hearing it, become confused and do not understand. All the Śrāvakas and Pratyekabuddhas are unable to comprehend this sutra.

"Śāriputra, you too only enter this sutra through faith—how much more so for other Śrāvakas? Moreover, Śāriputra, you should not teach this sutra to the arrogant, the lazy, those with the view of self, nor to those common folk of shallow understanding who are deeply attached to the five desires. For those who do not believe and slander this sutra shall reap grave karmic consequences."

---

## SCHOLARLY APPARATUS

### Footnote Commentary

1. **"Lost the infinite wisdom of the Tathāgata"**: This phrase expresses Śāriputra's regret at believing he has achieved the ultimate when in fact he has only attained the intermediate goal of Arhatship. The "infinite wisdom" represents the comprehensive Buddha-wisdom that transcends what the disciples believed they had attained.

2. **"Born from the Buddha's mouth, born from the dharma"** (，): This metaphor suggests that the true nature of discipleship is not merely intellectual understanding but transformation through the Buddha's teaching. The "birth" from the Buddha's mouth parallels Brahma's creation of being from primordial sound (Vāc), linking Buddhist teaching to broader mythological frameworks of creation through utterance.

3. **"Protected and remembered by all the Buddhas"**: The phrase (protect and remember) suggests that the Lotus Sutra is not merely historical but receives the active support and endorsement of all Buddhas. This establishes the text's authority as universally recognized among enlightened beings.

4. **"The land named Pure Radiance"**: The land name (separating from defilement) symbolizes the absence of the five corruptions and suggests a realm of perfect purity. The seven-jeweled trees bearing eternal flowers symbolize the unending abundance of enlightened reality.

5. **"The great jewels of that realm are the Bodhisattvas themselves"**: This remarkable statement reverses conventional value hierarchies—typically, material treasures constitute wealth, but here enlightened beings are the true wealth. This reflects the Buddhist teaching that sentient beings with spiritual potential are the most precious resource in existence.

 *Philosophical Significance*: This inversion of value systems represents a fundamental reorientation of human priorities toward spiritual development and awakening capacity.

6. **"These Bodhisattvas... have long planted roots of virtue"**: The phrase (roots of virtue) employs botanical metaphor to suggest that enlightenment grows from deep roots established through countless ages of cultivation. This implies continuity of consciousness across rebirths and the accumulative nature of spiritual development.

7. **"The three-world decaying house"**: The metaphor of the three-world house is one of the most famous in Buddhist literature. The three worlds (desire realm, form realm, formless realm) represent all conditioned existence. The decaying condition symbolizes impermanence; the fire symbolizes affliction.

 *Cosmological Significance*: This image represents the Buddhist rejection of cyclical perpetuation within samsara and the imperative toward transcendence.

8. **"The pain is acute, yet their minds harbor no loathing"** (，): This psychological observation illustrates the Buddhist principle of avidyā (ignorance)—sentient beings suffer but fail to recognize suffering or generate the will to transcend it. This is the fundamental problematic condition that necessitates the Buddha's skillful teaching.

9. **"Running about in play to the east and west, glancing at their father"** (、): The children's absorption in play despite danger illustrates how sentient beings become trapped in habitual patterns of craving and distraction. Their occasional glances at the father represent moments of potential awakening that are not yet actualized.

10. **"Rare and wonderful playthings... suited to their desires"** (，): The father's knowledge of each child's preferences represents the Buddha's understanding of individual sentient beings' capacities and predispositions. This emphasizes personalized pedagogical adaptation.

11. **"Seated in the open space at the four crossroads"** (、): The four crossroads (or four directions) represent universal accessibility. The open space represents freedom from enclosure. The safety of this place symbolizes the security of having escaped conditioned existence.

12. **"Its ground shall be made of lapis lazuli, with eight crossways"** (，): The lapis lazuli represents transcendent beauty and purity. The eight crossways (eight directions/eight paths) reference the Noble Eightfold Path and suggest comprehensive accessibility and completeness.

13. **"Each of the children, riding the great carriage, experienced what they had never before experienced"** (，): The children's surprise at receiving the great carriage rather than the small ones they expected illustrates the principle of exceeding expectations. The Lotus Sutra teaches that enlightenment surpasses what practitioners thought they were seeking.

 *Metaphysical Principle*: This represents the idea that ultimate realization transcends and includes what beings thought they were pursuing, rather than contradicting it.

14. **"Is the householder's giving of these precious great carts deceptive in any way?"**: The Buddha's question prompts the disciple to articulate the logic of skillful means—that adaptation for the purpose of liberation is not deceptive, even if not literal.

15. **"Even if the householder had not given them even the smallest single cart"** (，，): This profound statement suggests that the core issue is not the material reward but the rescue from danger. The gift of the carriage, while generous, is secondary to the fundamental act of liberation.

 *Ethical Significance*: This teaching reverses utilitarian logic by asserting that the value lies in the rescue itself, independent of subsequent rewards.

16. **"The Tathāgata is likewise the father of all the world"** (，): This explicit equation establishes the metaphor's application. The Tathāgata, like the householder, possesses comprehensive wisdom of sentient beings' conditions and capacities.

17. **"Never ceasing in endeavor, constantly seeking to benefit all beings"** (，，): This characterization emphasizes the Tathāgata's unwearying engagement with all beings' liberation. The phrase suggests that enlightenment does not withdraw from the world but increases engagement.

18. **"The fire of the three poisons (greed, hatred, delusion)"**: The "three poisons" (tri-dosa) are the fundamental defilements in Buddhist psychology. Their characterization as fire suggests their burning, consuming, destructive nature within consciousness.

19. **"Enable them to engage in play"**: The concept of play (yóuxì) here represents liberation as a state of freedom, creativity, and joy rather than grim asceticism. This suggests enlightenment as a positive state of flourishing rather than mere cessation.

20. **"How could they comprehend Buddha-wisdom?"**: This rhetorical question establishes the pedagogical principle—direct teaching of ultimate truth to those still entrapped in suffering is ineffective because the necessary preconditions for understanding do not exist.

 *Epistemological Significance*: This reflects the Buddhist principle that understanding requires both intellectual preparation and experiential readiness.

21. **"Teaching the three vehicles"** (、、、): The Buddha explicitly teaches the three vehicles as adaptations of the One Buddha Vehicle. This represents the most explicit statement of the Lotus Sutra's central teaching.

22. **"All the Buddhas' praise, free and unbound, with nothing to depend upon or seek"** (，，): These phrases describe the teaching's qualities—it is endorsed by enlightened beings, leads to freedom, and liberation, and creates independence from external props.

23. **"The Śrāvaka Vehicle, like those children who sought the sheep-carts"** (，): The association of each vehicle with specific carts creates a memorable symbolic structure. The sheep-cart represents the gentler, more accessible path.

24. **"The Pratyekabuddha Vehicle, like those children who sought the deer-carts"** (，): The deer-cart represents the more rapid, graceful path of self-enlightenment through understanding causes and conditions.

25. **"The Great Vehicle... like those children who sought the ox-carts"** (，，): The ox-cart represents the strongest, most comprehensive vehicle capable of bearing all beings toward enlightenment. The ox's strength and endurance symbolize the bodhisattva's capacity for sustained altruistic effort.

26. **"All of which are of one characteristic, one type"** (、): This statement emphasizes the essential identity of all paths despite their apparent differences. The "one characteristic" and "one type" refer to the underlying unified reality beneath phenomenal variety.

27. **"Only they do not all have the capacity to receive it"**: This crucial qualifier distinguishes between the Tathāgata's unlimited capacity to grant and sentient beings' limited capacity to receive. The barrier is not the teaching but the recipients' readiness.

28. **"The Buddhas, through the power of skillful means, within the One Buddha Vehicle, make distinctions and teach the three vehicles"** (，、): This is the explicit formula summarizing the entire parable. It states that the three vehicles are not ultimately real but pedagogical distinctions within the One Vehicle.

 *Metaphysical Innovation*: This represents a revolutionary reconceptualization of Buddhist teaching hierarchy, dissolving apparent contradictions by reframing them as skillful adaptations.

29. **Warnings regarding rejection of the Lotus Sutra**: The extensive verses warning of grave karmic consequences for those who reject or slander the Lotus Sutra represent a significant escalation of stakes. The severe punishment described (rebirth in hell, as animals, with physical deformities) emphasizes the sutra's ultimate importance while raising questions about karmic determinism.

 *Hermeneutical Note*: These warnings should be understood not as mere threats but as descriptions of natural karmic consequences of rejecting the path to enlightenment—the continued suffering that results from failing to heed the Buddha's guidance toward liberation.

---

### Philosophical Implications
### Key Philosophical Insights from Chapter 3:

### The Nature of Skilful Means (Upaya)
The burning house parable demonstrates that skillful means is not deception but appropriate adaptation. The father's initial promise of three carts and subsequent gift of one great carriage illustrates how teachings that appear contradictory are actually expressions of a unified purpose.**Contemporary Application**: In systems thinking and organizational theory, this principle parallels the understanding that different communication strategies serve a unified goal of transmitting understanding.**Metaphysical Unity Beneath Apparent Multiplicity**
The three vehicles are not genuinely different paths but appear different from the perspective of beings with different capacities. The underlying reality is the One Buddha Vehicle.**Philosophical Parallel**: This reflects Neoplatonic emanationism and Hindu Advaita Vedanta's assertion of ultimate non-duality beneath apparent multiplicity.**The Priority of Liberation Over Literal Truth**
The householder's promise of small carts followed by the gift of great carriages exemplifies the principle that the means of achieving liberation takes priority over literal truthfulness. This represents a profound reordering of ethical priorities.**The Buddha's Infinite Compassion**
The Buddha's sustained effort to teach beings who "neither startled nor afraid" demonstrates compassion that does not withdraw despite resistance. This suggests enlightenment as eternally engaged with sentient suffering.

### Apparatus Summary

**Key Philosophical Concepts**: Skillful means, Metaphysical unity, Priority of liberation, Buddha's infinite compassion

**Cross-Chapter Connections**:
- **Chapter 1**: Introduces universal assembly and Buddha-nature
- **Chapter 2**: Develops the principle of skillful means
- **Chapter 3**: Illustrates skillful means through the burning house parable
- **Chapter 4**: Shows disciples' acceptance of the teaching through the prodigal son parable
- **Chapters 5-7**: Develop specific applications of these principles
- **Chapter 16**: Cosmological extension of the teaching to Buddha's eternal nature

---

*End of Chapter 3*
---

### Analytical Commentary (Moved from Text)

**Analysis 1**: The Buddha then spoke extensive verses warning of the grave consequences of rejecting the Lotus Sutra while also affirming those who receive it with faith. The teaching concludes with criteria for worthy recipients:[^29]

**Analysis 2**: The Buddha then specifies eighteen categories of people who are suitable recipients of the Lotus Sutra, including those of keen faculties, those who serve the Buddha with sincere effort, those who guard pure precepts, and those who seek the great vehicle with single-minded devotion.

# Chapter 4: Faith And Understanding

## The Four Great Disciples' Joy and Recognition

At that time, the wise Subhūti, Mahākātyāyana, Mahākāśyapa, and Mahāmaudgalyāyana, having heard from the Buddha this unprecedented teaching, with the World-Honored One granting prophecy of anuttara-samyak-saṃbodhi to Śāriputra, their hearts were moved with wonder and delight. They immediately rose from their seats, straightened their robes, uncovered their right shoulders in respectful posture, knelt upon their right knees, unified their minds by placing their palms together in reverent salutation, bowed deeply in respect, gazed upon the Buddha's countenance with undivided attention, and addressed the Buddha:

"World-Honored One, we are elders among the assembled monks, advanced in age, and we have long believed that we had already attained nirvāṇa and had no further capacity for spiritual attainment. We harbored no aspiration to advance toward anuttara-samyak-saṃbodhi.

"World-Honored One, in ages past when you taught the dharma at length, we sat in the assembly, but our bodies were weary and lethargic. We contemplated only emptiness, signlessness, and non-activity. Regarding the Bodhisattva dharma—the display of supernatural powers, the purification of Buddha-lands, and the perfection of sentient beings—our hearts did not delight in these teachings. Why was this?

"The World-Honored One caused us to escape from the three worlds and attain the realization of nirvāṇa. Moreover, at this time we are advanced in age. Toward the Buddha's teaching and transformation of Bodhisattvas aspiring to anuttara-samyak-saṃbodhi, we have not generated even a single thought of joy.

"Yet now, before the Buddha, we have heard the prophecy that we too shall attain anuttara-samyak-saṃbodhi as Śrāvakas (voice-hearers). Our hearts are extremely joyful—we have experienced what we had never experienced before. We did not anticipate that at this time we would suddenly hear such a rare teaching. We deeply rejoice and feel that we have obtained great benefit, acquiring precious treasures without having sought them."[^1]

---

## The Parable of the Prodigal Son: Introduction

"World-Honored One, we now wish to speak in parable to clarify this meaning:

"There was a man who, in his youth, ignorant and without understanding, abandoned his father and fled, dwelling in another land for a long time—ten, twenty, or even fifty years.[^2] In his advancing age, he became impoverished and, wandering in all directions seeking clothing and food, gradually traveled and eventually arrived near his native land.

"His father, having long sought to find his son without success, had settled in a certain city. His household was immensely wealthy, with treasures beyond measure—gold, silver, lapis lazuli, coral, amber, crystal, pearls—all his storehouses completely filled and overflowing. He had numerous servants, officials, retainers, and common people. He possessed countless elephants, horses, chariots, ox-carts, and sheep. His wealth was engaged in business, spreading throughout other lands, and there were many merchants and traders. His father's thoughts constantly turned to his lost son.

"At that time, the impoverished son, seeking employment and wandering through villages and traveling through districts, eventually arrived at the city where his father had settled. His father had long mourned his son, and he and his son had been separated for more than fifty years. Yet the father never spoke of this matter to others. He merely thought within himself, harboring regret in his heart. He contemplated: 'I am old and approaching the end of life. I possess abundant wealth and treasures—gold, silver, precious jewels—my storehouses are completely full. But I have no son to whom I can entrust these riches. One day I shall die, and my wealth shall scatter and be lost with nothing to which it can be consigned. This is why I am constantly concerned and every moment remember my son.'

"Furthermore, he formed this thought: 'If I could only obtain my son and entrust him with my wealth, I would be at ease and happiness, without further worries.'"[^3]

---

## The Encounter: The Son Becomes Afraid

"World-Honored One, at that time the impoverished son, having hired himself to various employers, eventually arrived at his father's dwelling. He stood beside the gate. From a distance, he saw his father, seated upon a magnificent throne, his feet resting upon a precious footrest. Brahmins, nobles, and merchants all paid him respectful reverence. His body was adorned with pearls and precious ornaments worth thousands of millions of coins. Officials, servants, and retainers held white fly-whisks, standing to his left and right. A jeweled canopy was suspended above him, with precious flower banners hanging down. Fragrant water had been sprinkled on the ground, and precious flowers were scattered about. Precious objects were arrayed in display, brought in and taken out for distribution. Such was the variety of his magnificent adornment, and his authority and dignity were specially exalted.

"The impoverished son, seeing his father possessed of such great power and majesty, immediately became filled with fear and regret for having come to this place. He secretly formed this thought:

"'This man may be a king or the equal of a king. He is certainly not a place where I can earn a livelihood through manual labor. It would be better for me to go to a poor village where I might have a place to exert my strength, where food and clothing can be easily obtained. If I remain here for a long time, I might be constrained and forced to labor.'

"Forming this thought, he hastily fled."[^4]

---

## The Father's Recognition and Gentle Retrieval

"At that time the wealthy householder, seated upon his throne, recognized his son immediately and his heart was filled with great joy. He formed this thought:

"'Now I have someone to whom I can entrust my wealth and treasures. I have constantly thought of this son, yet had no means to see him. Unexpectedly, he has come—this accords perfectly with my wish. Although I am advanced in age, my desires remain strong.'

"He immediately sent attendants to pursue and bring back his son. At that moment, the messenger hastily ran after the poor son to capture him. The poor son, startled and alarmed, cried out in protest:

"'I have committed no offense against you—why do you seize me?'

"The messenger held him even more tightly and forcibly dragged him back. At that time, the poor son, thinking that he had committed no transgression yet was being imprisoned and seized, believed his death was certain, and his terror increased. He became unconscious and collapsed to the ground.

"The father, observing this from a distance, instructed the messenger:

"'Release this person! Do not forcibly bring him. Sprinkle cold water on his face to revive him, and do not speak to him further.'

"Why was this? The father understood that his son, possessed of inferior spiritual capacity, was intimidated by the father's power and wealth, which the son found difficult to comprehend. Although the father clearly knew this was indeed his son, he employed skilful means, not telling other people that this was his son.

"The messenger told the son:

"'I now release you. Go wherever you wish.'

"The poor son was delighted to obtain what he had not expected. He rose from the ground and went to a poor village, seeking employment and food."[^5]

---

## The Father's Strategy: Gradual Approach

"At that time, the householder, desiring to entice and guide his son through the employment of skilful means, secretly sent two people of humble appearance, without power or prestige:

"'You may go to that place and gradually speak to the poor son, saying: "This place has work available. We shall pay you double wages. If the poor son accepts, bring him here and have him work. If he asks what kind of work it is, tell him: 'We shall hire you to clean excrement. We two shall also work with you.'"'

"The two messengers immediately sought out the poor son, and having found him, explained everything in detail. At that time, the poor son, first receiving payment, immediately began cleaning excrement with them.

"His father, observing his son, felt compassion and was amazed.
